            What kind of a drinker are YOU? From the Nutty Professor to the Mary Poppins, psychologists reveal how our personalities change when we've had a few
           </span>
          </a>
         </li>

          <a href="/health/article-3160730/Drugs-damage-women-s-brains-men-s-Cocaine-abuse-affects-areas-control-emotions-decision-making.html">

       <div class="article-text wide" id="js-article-text">
        <h1>
         ASK THE DOCTOR: A blocked artery in my neck caused a mini stroke
        </h1>
        <p class="author-section mol-para-with-font byline-plain">
         By
         <a class="author" href="/debate/columnists/columnist-1068170/Martin-Scurr-for-the-Daily-Mail.html" rel="nofollow">
          Martin Scurr for the Daily Mail
         </a>
        </p>
        <p class="byline-section mol-para-with-font">
         <span class="article-timestamp article-timestamp-published">
          <span class="article-timestamp-label">
           Published:
          </span>
          19:17 EST, 28 July 2014
         </span>
         |
         <span class="article-timestamp article-timestamp-updated">
          <span class="article-timestamp-label">
           Updated:
          </span>
          19:17 EST, 28 July 2014
         </span>
        </p>
        <div id="articleIconLinksContainer">

            <a>

           </span>
          </li>
          <li class="share-email">

           </a>
          </li>
          <li class="share-icons" id="shareLinkTop">
           <a class="js-sl share-link" href="#socialLinks">

        <br/>
        <div class="floatRHS">
         <img alt="The usual term for what Derek have experienced is a transient ischaemic attack - sometimes referred to as a 'mini stroke'" class="blkBorder img-share" height="423" id="i-187895ee35d1bff2" src="http://i.dailymail.co.uk/i/pix/2014/07/29/article-2708906-04FDBC62000005DC-100_306x423.jpg" width="306"/>
         <p class="imageCaption">
          The usual term for what Derek have experienced is a transient ischaemic attack - sometimes referred to as a 'mini stroke'
         </p>
        </div>
        <p>
        </p>
        <p class="mol-para-with-font">
         <font color="00B1A6" style="font-size:1.6em;">
          After
 a transient ischaemic stroke I have had an ultrasound, which has shown 
that the main artery in my neck is 90 per cent blocked. The options are a
 stent or open surgery. I would like your opinion on which is the best 
and safest to go for.
         </font>
        </p>
        <p class="mol-para-with-font">
         <font style="font-size:1.6em;">
          Derek Thomson, Newcastle.
         </font>
        </p>
        <p class="mol-para-with-font">
         <font style="font-size: 1.2em;">
          The usual term for
 what you have experienced is a transient ischaemic attack - sometimes 
referred to as a 'mini stroke'. These are common - nearly every week, 
I'll see a patient, typically someone in their 50s or 60s, who's had 
one.
         </font>
        </p>
        <p class="mol-para-with-font">
         <font style="font-size: 1.2em;">
          A transient ischaemic attack (TIA) occurs when the blood flow 
through part of the brain is disrupted briefly. The lack of oxygen to 
the brain causes symptoms similar to those of a stroke, such as speech 
or visual disturbance, or numbness or weakness in the arms or legs.
         </font>
        </p>
        <p class="mol-para-with-font">
         <font style="font-size: 1.2em;">
          The
 key difference is that the symptoms of a TIA don't last long, typically
 only minutes, and are certainly completely gone within 24 hours, 
because there is no permanent damage to the brain.
          <br/>
          The most common 
cause is a small blood clot that has travelled up one of the two carotid
 arteries supplying blood from the heart to the brain, causing a brief 
blockage.
          <br/>
         </font>
        </p>
        <p class="mol-para-with-font">
         <font style="font-size: 1.2em;">
          These clots typically form on fatty build-ups (plaque) in blood vessels.
          <br/>
         </font>
        </p>
        <p class="mol-para-with-font">
         <font style="font-size: 1.2em;">
          Fragments of the clot then break away and temporarily block the blood vessels.
          <br/>
         </font>
        </p>
        <p class="mol-para-with-font">
         <font style="font-size: 1.2em;">
          The
 clots may also come about as a result of atrial fibrillation, where the
 heart beats irregularly - allowing the blood to pool and clot.
         </font>
        </p>
        <p class="mol-para-with-font">
         <font style="font-size: 1.2em;">
          About 10 per cent of people who've had a transient ischaemic attack go on to have a stroke within a month without treatment.
          <br/>
         </font>
        </p>
        <p class="mol-para-with-font">
         <font style="font-size: 1.2em;">
          That is why you had the ultrasound scan to pin down the exact cause, so that preventative action can be taken.
         </font>
        </p>
        <p class="mol-para-with-font">
        </p>
        <div class="related-carousel with-fb health half" data-track-module="am-related_carousel^related_carousel" data-track-pos="static" data-track-selector=".rotator-panels a:not([class*=external])" id="p-16">
         <div class="rotator bdrcc">
          <div class="rotator-title">

           </ul>
          </div>
          <ul class="rotator-panels link-bogr1 linkro-ccox">

             <img alt="Pomegranate peel is rich in antioxidants" height="60" src="http://i.dailymail.co.uk/i/pix/2014/07/29/article-2708876-1C1FA0BD00000578-713_87x84.jpg" width="62"/>
             <span>
              Fruit that makes ice cream healthy: Pomegranate makes sweet...
             </span>

            <a href="/health/article-2708914/ME-AND-MY-OPERATION-Pacemaker-tum-eases-heartburn.html">
             <img alt="Chronic heartburn can now be tackled with an implant in the gullet" height="60" src="http://i.dailymail.co.uk/i/pix/2014/07/29/article-2708914-200EA34A00000578-765_87x84.jpg" width="62"/>
             <span>
              ME AND MY OPERATION: Pacemaker for your tum that eases...
             </span>
            </a>
           </li>
           <li>
            <a href="/health/article-2708895/My-vices-cheese-red-wine-Phil-Spencer-microscope.html">
             <img alt="TV presenter and property expert Phil Spencer" height="60" src="http://i.dailymail.co.uk/i/pix/2014/07/29/article-2708895-200B1EC400000578-940_87x84.jpg" width="62"/>
             <span>
              'My vices are cheese and red wine': Phil Spencer under the...

         <div class="fb">
          <h2>
           Share this article
          </h2>
          <div class="share">
           <span class="wai">
            Share
           </span>
          </div>
         </div>
        </div>
        <script type="text/javascript">
         DM.later('bundle', function(){
   DM.has('p-16', 'Rotator', {
     pageSize: 1,
     pageCount: 2.0,
     activeClass:  'active',
     rotate: false
   });
   $('#p-16 .share').on('click', function() {
     DMS.Facebook.postToFB('related');
   });
 });
        </script>
        <p class="mol-para-with-font">
         <font style="font-size: 1.2em;">
          This revealed a narrowing in one or both of your carotid arteries in your neck.
          <br/>
         </font>
        </p>
        <p class="mol-para-with-font">
         <font style="font-size: 1.2em;">
          The
 risk is that the fatty deposits clogging the lining could cause another
 clot or blockage, which could lead to another TIA or a stroke, so 
treatment of some form is necessary.
         </font>
        </p>
        <p class="mol-para-with-font">
         <font style="font-size: 1.2em;">
          You will have already been 
prescribed a drug - probably aspirin - to make your blood cells less 
likely to stick together and clot.
          <br/>
         </font>
        </p>
        <p class="mol-para-with-font">
         <font style="font-size: 1.2em;">
          You will also have been 
prescribed a statin to lower cholesterol levels to help minimise future 
blockage of the arteries; the statin will also make the clots on the 
fatty plaque more stable and less likely to break off.
          <br/>
         </font>
        </p>
        <p class="mol-para-with-font">
         <font style="font-size: 1.2em;">
          These drugs -
 when combined with good blood pressure control - are the most effective
 treatment for patients with minor blockages.
         </font>
        </p>
        <div class="clear">
        </div>
        <div class="artSplitter">
         <img alt="About 10 per cent of people who've had a transient ischaemic attack go on to have a stroke within a month" class="blkBorder img-share" height="337" id="i-6ad07add77ec17f9" src="http://i.dailymail.co.uk/i/pix/2014/07/29/article-2708906-0CBC1D0A000005DC-953_634x337.jpg" width="634"/>
         <p class="imageCaption">
          About 10 per cent of people who've had a transient ischaemic attack go on to have a stroke within a month
         </p>
        </div>
        <p>
        </p>
        <p class="mol-para-with-font">
         <font style="font-size: 1.2em;">
          However, for more 
severe blockages, patients benefit from having surgery to clear out the 
blockages or to implant a stent - a fine metal tube - to open up the 
artery.
         </font>
        </p>
        <p class="mol-para-with-font">
         <font style="font-size: 1.2em;">
          With the first procedure, known as a carotid endarterectomy, 
an incision is made between the jaw and the breastbone and the artery 
opened up, so that material clogging the artery can be removed - this 
is done under local or general anaesthetic.
          <br/>
         </font>
        </p>
        <p class="mol-para-with-font">
         <font style="font-size: 1.2em;">
          It reduces the risk of 
stroke by about 50 per cent in those who have had a TIA and who have a 
narrowing of more than 70 per cent. The risk of the operation causing a 
stroke is less than 5 per cent.
         </font>
        </p>
        <p class="mol-para-with-font">
         <font style="font-size: 1.2em;">
          Putting in a stent is less invasive.
 The stent can be inserted via a small incision in the leg into a blood 
vessel and pushed up to the artery in the neck.
          <br/>
         </font>
        </p>
        <p class="mol-para-with-font">
         <font style="font-size: 1.2em;">
          The process is 
carried out under local anaesthetic and is as safe and effective as the 
endarterectomy, but far less dramatic and frightening.
          <br/>
         </font>
        </p>
        <p class="mol-para-with-font">
         <font style="font-size: 1.2em;">
          It seems a good option to go for, if you are given the choice.
         </font>
        </p>
        <p class="mol-para-with-font">
         <font color="00B1A6" style="font-size:1.6em;">
          M
          <font color="00B1A6">
           y
          </font>
          11-year-old son is very sporty, but for the past 18 months has suffered
 from crippling headaches when exerting himself in warm conditions (even
 when hydrated), which forces him to stop. He gets very red in the face, but doesn't appear to sweat. Our
 GP says the headaches sound like migraine attacks and wants to 
prescribe daily preventative medication, but I feel this should be used 
as a last resort because of his age.
         </font>
        </p>
        <p class="mol-para-with-font">
         <font style="font-size:1.6em;">
          Zoe Brown, Brentwood, Essex.
         </font>
        </p>
        <p class="mol-para-with-font">
         <font style="font-size: 1.2em;">
          Up to 10 per cent of people find that intense physical activity can trigger a bad headache, so your son is far from alone.
          <br/>
         </font>
        </p>
        <p class="mol-para-with-font">
         <font style="font-size: 1.2em;">
          Sixteen
 per cent of us will develop migraine at some stage in life; and it's 
not just a condition that affects adults, with up to 5 per cent of 
children suffering from them.
          <br/>
         </font>
        </p>
        <p class="mol-para-with-font">
         <font style="font-size: 1.2em;">
          Quite why exercise might trigger 
migraines is not clear - indeed, regular physical exercise is also known
 to help reduce the frequency and intensity of migraine headaches, 
possibly because it triggers the release of feel-good endorphins.
         </font>
        </p>
        <p class="mol-para-with-font">
         <font style="font-size: 1.2em;">
          It may be that endorphins can also have a negative effect, but the chemistry of this is as yet poorly understood.
          <br/>
         </font>
        </p>
        <p class="mol-para-with-font">
         <font style="font-size: 1.2em;">
          Your GP has recognised the diagnosis, and perhaps the best move would be a trial of prevention medication.
          <br/>
         </font>
        </p>
        <p class="mol-para-with-font">
         <font style="font-size: 1.2em;">
          I
 totally understand your reservations about this, but the medication 
does not have to be taken on a daily basis, only prior to engaging in 
vigorous sports.
          <br/>
         </font>
        </p>
        <p class="mol-para-with-font">
         <font style="font-size: 1.2em;">
          For many years, the beta blocker propranolol - 
typically used to treat blood pressure - has been used for this with 
good effect.
          <br/>
         </font>
        </p>
        <div class="clear">
        </div>
        <div class="artSplitter">
         <img alt="Up to 10 per cent of people find that intense physical activity can trigger a bad headache" class="blkBorder img-share" height="480" id="i-5a0caabf332d0af0" src="http://i.dailymail.co.uk/i/pix/2014/07/29/article-2708906-12ACB416000005DC-555_634x480.jpg" width="634"/>
         <p class="imageCaption">
          Up to 10 per cent of people find that intense physical activity can trigger a bad headache
         </p>
        </div>
        <p>
        </p>
        <p class="mol-para-with-font">
         <font style="font-size: 1.2em;">
          The smallest tablet size is 10mg, though the dose range
 does extend up to 160mg in adults and, generally, the side-effects, if 
any, are minimal as the drugs are safe and well tolerated.
          <br/>
         </font>
        </p>
        <p class="mol-para-with-font">
         <font style="font-size: 1.2em;">
          However,
 beta blockers are not advised for those with asthma, and another 
unwanted effect can be fatigue, although the doses needed for migraine 
prevention tend to be very much smaller than the doses used for 
hypertension.
          <br/>
         </font>
        </p>
        <p class="mol-para-with-font">
         <font style="font-size: 1.2em;">
          A 10mg tablet half an hour before physical exertion may prevent your son's headache occurring.
         </font>
        </p>
        <p class="mol-para-with-font">
         <font style="font-size: 1.2em;">
          If
 this works, it proves the diagnosis of exertional headache, also known 
as exercise-induced migraine, and so I would suggest a further 
consultation with your GP to consider prescribing this for a trial.
          <br/>
         </font>
        </p>
        <p class="mol-para-with-font">
         <font style="font-size: 1.2em;">
          I'm sure that is what he had in mind, and I hope that this will reassure you about your concerns over safety and side-effects.
          <br/>
         </font>
        </p>
        <p class="mol-para-with-font">
         <font style="font-size: 1.2em;">
          It would be a similar treatment for adults affected in this way, with a 10mg or higher dose prior to sports.
          <br/>
         </font>
        </p>
        <p class="mol-para-with-font">
         <font style="font-size: 1.2em;">
          In
 some competitive sports at professional level, beta blockers are not 
permitted - an alternative is the anti-inflammatory drug, indomethacin -
 a 25mg or 50mg dose.
          <br/>
         </font>
        </p>
        <p class="mol-para-with-font">
         <font style="font-size: 1.2em;">
          This can be as effective and isn't banned at any competitive level.
         </font>
        </p>
        <div class="floatRHS art-ins health">
         <h3 class="wocc">
          <b>
           <font style="font-size:1.2em;">
            Write to Dr Scurr
            <br/>
           </font>
          </b>
         </h3>
         <div class="ins cleared xolcc bdrcc">
          <b>
           <font style="font-size:1.2em;">
           </font>
          </b>
          <p>
           <b>
             To contact Dr Scurr with a health query, write to him at Good Health Daily Mail, 2 Derry Street, London W8 5TT or email drmartin@dailymail.co. uk - including contact details.
            <br/>
            <br/>
            Dr Scurr cannot enter into personal correspondence. His replies cannot apply to individual cases and should be taken in a general context. Always consult your own GP with any health worries.
           </b>
          </p>
          <b>
          </b>
         </div>
        </div>
        <h2>
         <font style="font-size: 1.2em;">
          By the way...Sleep is vital for your brain
         </font>
        </h2>
        <p class="mol-para-with-font">
         <font style="font-size: 1.2em;">
          I reckon that I never get enough sleep.
         </font>
        </p>
        <p class="mol-para-with-font">
         <font style="font-size: 1.2em;">
          This
 is partly as a result of poor self-discipline - call me a workaholic - 
and partly because of the medication I take (corticosteroid, but more on
 that another time).
          <br/>
         </font>
        </p>
        <p class="mol-para-with-font">
         <font style="font-size: 1.2em;">
          I probably average around five hours each 
night; without sufficient sleep any of us will feel chronically tired 
and irritable, function less efficiently, find ourselves more prone to 
infection, and - if you're like me - suffer from more frequent 
migraines. However, recently I had something of wake-up call, as it 
were.
          <br/>
         </font>
        </p>
        <p class="mol-para-with-font">
         <font style="font-size: 1.2em;">
          A new study has suggested that our need for sleep is triggered by a build-up of toxins in the brain during the day.
         </font>
        </p>
        <p class="mol-para-with-font">
         <font style="font-size: 1.2em;">
          Sleeping allows these damaging toxins to be cleared.
         </font>
        </p>
        <p class="mol-para-with-font">
         <font style="font-size: 1.2em;">
          It
 seems that when we sleep there is a greater flow of the cerebrospinal 
fluid - the watery, salty substance that circulates around the brain and
 down around the spinal cord - that washes away waste products from 
crucial brain cells. That's why sleep is so essential. Of course, we 
need more research into this area - but it's worth noting that we've 
only recently come to realise that physical exercise protects against 
dementia, and it's the only known human activity that's proven to do so.
         </font>
        </p>
        <p class="mol-para-with-font">
         <font style="font-size: 1.2em;">
          I'm now beginning to wonder if I should also be talking to my patients about getting enough sleep.
          <br/>
         </font>
        </p>
        <p class="mol-para-with-font">
         <font style="font-size: 1.2em;">
          Yet another life enhancement that costs nothing, though it does need a little application and self-discipline.
         </font>
        </p>
        <div class="thinCenter">
         <font style="font-size:1.2em;">
         </font>
        </div>
        <p class="mol-para-with-font">
        </p>

       <script async="" src="//cdn.taboola.com/libtrc/dailymail-us/loader.js">
       </script>
       <script type="text/javascript">
        DM.later('bundle', function(){
        var n = 0;
        var taboola_every_n = 10;
        var taboola_counter = taboola_every_n - 1;

        var addTaboola = function (node){
            var id = "taboola-stream-thumbnails-" + (++n);
            var taboola_node = '&lt;div id="' + id + '"&gt;&lt;/div&gt;';
            $(node).after(taboola_node);

            window._taboola = window._taboola || [];
            _taboola.push({
                mode: 'thumbnails-b',
                container: id,
                placement: "Stream Thumbnails " + n});
        };

        DM.has('infinite-list', 'InfiniteList', {
url: '/api/infinite-list.html?channelShortName=health&amp;pageSize=10',
            total: 17.0,
            from: 0,
            onAfterAppend: function (container){
                var items = $(container).children('div');
                if(!taboola_every_n){
                    return;
                }

                items.each(function (){
                    if (taboola_counter == 0){
                        addTaboola(this);
                        taboola_counter = taboola_every_n - 1;
                    }
                    else {
                        taboola_counter--;
                    }
                });
            }
        });
    });
       </script>

                    message =  "We think our Android tablet app is a much better way of viewing MailOnline. Available for FREE on Google Play. Download it now!";
					expireDate.setMonth(expireDate.getMonth() + 1);
				}
			}
				var log;

				function setCookie(){
					DM.setCookie(cName, cName, expireDate, "/");
				}

				function getCookie(){
					return DM.getCookie(cName);
				}

				if(cName &amp;&amp; !getCookie()){
					var conf = confirm(message);
					
					if (conf){
						setCookie();
						window.location = link;
					} else {
						setCookie();
					}
				}
			
		});
         </script>
